McCollum","content":" The door was stuck, and C.J. McCollum had to go. There was a game to be played in less than 90 minutes, and pro players have their routines. But the door in one of the rooms near the Portland Trail Blazers\u2019 locker room at Moda Center was stuck, and the handle wouldn\u2019t move, either in the room or outside it, and McCollum had to go. Yet while someone fetched a key, the fifth-year guard chatted amiably with the strangers that had just interviewed him. It was in keeping with his character \u2014 a talented but humble kid from Canton, Ohio, that rose from unheralded and barely recruited out of high school to play at Lehigh University \u2014 an outstanding school, noted for its engineering program, in the Patriot League. Yet few engineers or Patriot League grads make in the NBA. McCollum, a journalism major (not many of those playing in the L, either), has made it, though. Averaging 22.1 points per game and shooting a seemingly unreal 54.7 percent on 3-pointers this season, McCollum and Damian Lillard are the unquestioned leaders of the Blazers and one of the top backcourts in the game, each capable of taking over at a moment\u2019s notice. They seek to raise the Blazers past the first round, where Portland\u2019s season has ended two of the last four seasons. With center Jusuf Nurkic around for a full season after last February\u2019s trade-deadline deal with Denver, the Portland has hopes they can be a top four seed. In the midst of the $106 million extension he got in 2016, McCollum also stepped out in the shoe game this summer, joining Dwyane Wade and his Portland teammate Evan Turner as an endorser with Li Ning, the Chinese shoe company, with a five-year deal. He continued preparing to take my job with his annual interview of Adam Silver . And, finally, someone managed to open the door. Me: Tell me how the season\u2019s gone so far. C.J. McCollum: I think we\u2019ve been up and down, honestly. Not finishing games at the end. The only convincing loss we\u2019ve had is that game with the Raptors, where it wasn\u2019t really close from the second quarter. But we had the buzzer beater with Blake (Griffin), and Giannis (Antetokounmpo) being Giannis, and the last game against the Utah Jazz, being up, what was it, up six with three minutes left , possession up five with two minutes left. Those are games we have to be able to win, but it\u2019s a good learning experience for us. In order to win games, you have to figure out how to get through them after you lose them. Me: The ball\u2019s in the right guys\u2019 hands at the end? CJM: Yeah. We\u2019ve got to be better. You\u2019re looking at the turnovers one game, then I miss a free throw (against the LA Clippers). It\u2019s uncharacteristic, but it can happen. You open the door up to great players, it\u2019s a league of very capable guys. And if they see they have a chance to win it, you never know what\u2019s going to happen. A guy may hit a three or just make a great shot down the stretch. Me: I know of coaches who literally walked the streets after losses, it was so hard on them. Do you stew after tough ones or do you have to flush them in order to be ready for the next one? CJM: It hurts. It hurts, especially when you\u2019re in position. I\u2019m a career, 80, 85 percent (free throw) shooter. Shot 91 last year. You go to the line, and you\u2019re thinking, this is cash, the game\u2019s over. You miss that first one, and literally after I missed it I said, man, they\u2019ve got a chance to hit a three here. You make the second free throw, and you\u2019re looking, you know Blake, you know Austin (Rivers), you know they\u2019re thinking \u2018no overtime; let\u2019s get this over with and go home.\u2019 And the three goes up, and all you\u2019re thinking is, one possession. Maybe you missed a shot; maybe you missed a defensive assignment. One possession is the difference in that game. Me: You and Dame, you\u2019ve taken possession of the team. So when you hit a little rough patch, do the two of you lead verbally, or is it just, let\u2019s both play better to get ourselves out of this? CJM: It\u2019s everything. We know independently, separate besides everything we do as a team, it\u2019s on us. The ball\u2019s in our hands the last five minutes. It\u2019s our decision to hit the pocket pass, hit the roll\/replace guy. It\u2019s a guard\u2019s game. Pick and roll-wise, you think about it, we\u2019re in every coverage offensively and defensively down the stretch, unless you\u2019re going against a team that\u2019s more post oriented. But for us, we\u2019ve just got to get better. We\u2019ve discussed how the season\u2019s going \u2026 it could be a lot worse. It could be a lot better. But it\u2019s good that this is happening now, get this out of the way. So when you get into November, December, and as the New Year comes around, you have all these learning experiences. And you have more play calls that you know can work down the stretch, and you\u2019re able to go to those. Me: You guys both recruited Carmelo hard in the summer . Did you ever think you had a real shot at any point? CJM: I mean, I figured why not? Why not recruit someone you think can help your team? I just told him the truth. I spend my summers in New York anyway \u2014 I\u2019m there with my girl, anyway \u2014 so seeing him work out in the same gym and having the same trainer, I didn\u2019t harp on it. I told him one time how he could help our team, and that was basically the end of it. I felt like when someone\u2019s a free agent and you know them and you\u2019re around them, you need to share how you think they can help you and how you think you can help them. They have the right to make their own decision, and I think he\u2019s happy where he\u2019s at, and I wish him the best except when they play us. ( Editor\u2019s Note: Anthony wasn\u2019t a free agent last summer, but he had approval power over any potential trades ) Me: So, they wound up keeping the team pretty intact from last year. Now, you can look at that two ways: they have confidence that our core group will continue to improve, or that we didn\u2019t do very much while other teams did, and we\u2019re further behind. Where do you fall? CJM: I think that we\u2019re getting better, collectively. You look at how we\u2019re performing \u2026 Our Defensive Rating is higher , and offensively, although we\u2019re not shooting the ball particularly well, we\u2019re still in the top 10 in offense. We\u2019re doing things the right way. We just have to close games out. Like I said before, the line between wins and losses is thin. It\u2019s one possession here, one possession there. We\u2019ve got to do a better job, especially at home. We\u2019ve played particularly well on the road, but those first halves, we\u2019ve had some slow starts. We\u2019ve got to get off to better start in these games. Me: It seems like a lot of teams are choppy. And I know it\u2019s that way usually at the beginning of the year. But do you think the fact that the preseason was shortened, and vets didn\u2019t get to go up and down the floor even for just a few minutes over five or six games like they used to has impacted that at all? CJM: I try not to make excuses. I feel like we know what the dates are ahead of time. You know when you need to be ready mentally and physically, and it\u2019s your job to do that \u2014 although I didn\u2019t play the first game (McCollum was suspended for the regular season opener after taking a couple of verrry small steps off the bench during a preseason game in October ). I was ready for the games after that, and I think the rest of the league (was) as well. You can look at it on the flip side and say that defenses should be worse. Guys shouldn\u2019t be in good shape defensively, and we should score more points. Because that means guys had less time to get together, a lot of new guys moving around different franchises and different teams. So we just have to do better collectively, and that\u2019s not just my team, all teams in general. I think you\u2019ll see the basketball and the level of play continue to rise as guys get more comfortable. Me: I wanted to ask you about your shoe deal with Li Ning, and how you researched that before you decided. What was important to you? CJM: A lot of things were important. I think the biggest thing is going somewhere where you\u2019re wanted and needed. I always say that mutual admiration. It\u2019s kind of how I chose Lehigh, and Lehigh chose me. It was a situation where we both felt like we could help each other, help one another, them being a big brand in a place like Asia, having that big influence and being able to help them branch out to the United States \u2014 more specifically, Portland, Oregon, and Canton, Ohio, and Bethlehem, Pennsylvania. Obviously those cities will become more familiar with the brand. Overall, I\u2019m thankful for the opportunity. As a kid growing up in Canton, Ohio, you never see yourself in a position to play in the NBA, much less have somebody pay you to wear shoes. It\u2019s a blessing in itself. I\u2019m just looking forward to the opportunity to represent a brand, and hopefully reach out and gain more information on them, and them gain more information on players in the United States, and little kids. Guys like DWade and ET (Evan Turner), guys who\u2019ve been in the brand before me, have been very helpful telling me about the brand, and informing me on ways to improve. Telling me about what China\u2019s going to be like, having gone there in the past. I\u2019m looking forward to it.  Me: I assume you talked with Errick about it as well? CJM: Yeah, I talked to my brother. He was in the CBA (Chinese Basketball Association) a few years ago, and with him being in the EuroLeague now with Efes (Pilson), I\u2019ve been very familiar with the CBA. Not only Li Ning, but Anta and the other brands as well. The research was in place, and I felt like the comfort was all that matters, being comfortable. I had my orthotics in place. I had shoes that were comfortable and to my liking. I always say, it\u2019s not the shoes; it\u2019s the player. If you can hoop, you can hoop. Me: That\u2019s true \u2014 but your feet are your career. CJM: They are. That\u2019s why it\u2019s important that you have the orthotics in place. I had another foot scan and I went through the process of having the molds in place, to where you need your feet, you need your orthotics. But having removable orthotics helps a lot, because you can take them out of different shoes. You just have to make sure they fit. Me: How big will it be having a full year of \u201cThe Beast\u201d (Nurkic) with you? CJM: We\u2019re looking forward to it. We\u2019ve gone through a full training camp now. He\u2019s more familiar with our play calling; more familiar with our culture and our chemistry. And the city in general. He\u2019s comfortable here. We\u2019re looking forward to doing something special \u2026 we\u2019ve got 74, 75 games left plus playoffs. We\u2019ll have to continue to either build on what we did at the end of last season, go forward, and when April comes around we\u2019ll be that much better. Me: Speaking of April: you\u2019ve been in the playoffs each of your first four seasons here, but you\u2019ve only gotten out of the first round once. How do you get over that hump? CJM: I think these close games we\u2019re losing right now, those are games we have to win to put ourselves in better position in the playoffs, be a better seed than the eighth seed. You don\u2019t want to be fighting in April, fighting for a spot. You want to have your spot solidified, so now you\u2019re working on rotations, everything\u2019s in place and you\u2019re trying to get healthy for the playoffs. That\u2019ll be the next step for us. We haven\u2019t been in that position in a few years where we\u2019re locked in early. You don\u2019t want to plan that last week out, and then be at the mercy of the schedule. So that\u2019s the goal. And then you go into the playoffs understanding that it\u2019s not going to be easy, that you have to win on the road and protect your home court. Me: So, what are you going to give me when AU beats Lehigh this year? CJM: I\u2019ll get you some Li Nings and a C.J. McCollum jersey. Me: Like that. And, in the unlikely, wholly unexpected event that Lehigh cheats and somehow beats AU, what would I have to get you? CJMcC: I see you got some Common Projects on . Give me some, and maybe a pocket square with some details. Me: Deal. And it\u2019s on tape. CJMcC: It\u2019s on tape. \u2014 Former 76ers GM Sam Hinkie ( @samhinkie ), Thursday, 12:23 p.m., referring to the Houston Astros\u2019 verrrry similar strategy to his own celebrated\/notorious \u201cProcess\u201d \u2014 using analytics combined with terrestrial information to maximize the odds of success in as short a time frame as possible. From Quintin Reed: Most teams are approaching the one-eighth pole of the regular season. Let\u2019s not make playoff plans just yet in Detroit. Andre Drummond\u2019s significant improvement so far from the foul line \u2014 75 percent so far this season after shooting 38.6 percent from there last season \u2014 could be a game-changer if he sustains anything close to it over 82 games. Coach Stan Van Gundy could not play Drummond down the stretch last season, such was his understandable fear of Hack-a-Drummond in the last few minutes of games. If that\u2019s no longer going to be an effective game-ending strategy for Pistons\u2019 opponents, Detroit\u2019s chances in close games should improve dramatically. I\u2019d think you\u2019d want the guy ranked first last season in Defensive Rating on the floor when it matters. Whether that was, in Jackson\u2019s case, a reported discussion of a deal ( and denied by Van Gundy ) last week for Eric Bledsoe in Phoenix, is up for debate. (The price point is almost always spot on, by the way.) And while the league shares best practices among its teams, I doubt it could or would want to mandate individual markets to give back. BY THE NUMBERS 13 \u2014 Consecutive times the Blazers have beaten the Lakers, a streak dating back to March 3, 2014, after Portland defeated L.A. in Portland on Thursday on Damian Lillard\u2019s last-second, tie-breaking three-pointer. 283 \u2014 Consecutive games, per ESPN Stats and Info, that the Clippers have lost when trailing by 20 or more points entering the fourth quarter. Los Angeles erased a 25-point fourth quarter deficit at home Sunday against the Heat and took a one-point lead in the final minute before falling 114-111. 1,156 \u2014  Career coaching victories for Gregg Popovich , who passed Phil Jackson Sunday for sixth place on the all-time victories list. Popovich is now 19 wins behind fifth-place George Karl and 54 behind fourth-place Pat Riley. 1) It was a pleasure to work Portland-Lakers Thursday and see two teams that, throughout the game, actually played through their big men and let them shoot and score in the post. Jusuf Nurkic scored 28 points for the Blazers; Brook Lopez had 27 for L.A. They scored on pick and roll dives, fadeaways, hooks and the occasional drop step. The world did not start spinning in the opposite direction. Cows did not begin singing opera. There were still 40 three-pointers taken in the game, including Damian Lillard\u2019s game-winner. It is possible, it seems, for centers to still contribute offensively in this league without having to go behind the arc. 2) Every once in a great while, things work out exactly how you thought they would. I thought Dwight Howard\u2019s remaining skills \u2014 rebounding, post defense, rim protection, screen setting and rolling \u2014 would be good ones in Charlotte, where Steve Clifford expects exertion on both ends of the floor. And so far, Howard has been outstanding for the Bugs, who are trying to weather injury to Nicolas Batum and work Michael Kidd-Gilchrist back into the lineup. 3) Good reads:  B\/R\u2019s Howard Beck on Nikola Jokic and BANG\u2019s Mark Medina on the story behind \u201cPopovich\/Kerr 2020\u201d . 4) The World Series was incredible theatre, incredible drama and incredible baseball. Kudos to both the champion Astros and the Dodgers for seven amazing games. 1) If you don\u2019t understand Draymond Green\u2019s issue with the word \u201cowner\u201d in the context of today\u2019s pro player protests, you\u2019re being willfully ignorant. No one begrudges Mark Cuban or anyone else whose intelligence, hard work and perseverance over a lifetime have left them in position to have enough capital to buy and then run a sports franchise. Nor does Green. To suggest that he and others uncomfortable with the word \u201cowner\u201d are doing so is unfair. How many times do you hear fans say \u2018well, he\/she owns the team; they can trade\/fire\/sign whoever they want?\u2019 I\u2019ve said that a few thousand times myself over the years. Yet I\u2019m also sensitive to the argument that the right of the franchise owner to make those decisions connotes control over the player\/person that is being signed\/traded\/fired, etc. And that has historic overtones in a nation where some still won\u2019t acknowledge the Civil War was fought over slavery. Recall what the late Dorothy Ewing, Patrick Ewing\u2019s mother, said to John Thompson, Jr., when the then-Hoyas coach was in the Ewing home to get the teenage boy\u2019s parents to sign the letter of intent that would commit Patrick Ewing to Georgetown. Thompson recalled in Sports Illustrated, years later: \u201cI asked her to sign. She looked at me and said, \u2018Mr. Thompson, you sign for land. You don\u2019t sign for people.\u2019 \u201d Everyone who works has a boss. You do, I do. ( My boss is named Tara . She\u2019s cool.) Now, how would you feel if someone said you have an owner instead of a boss? \u201cPete, this is Sam, your owner.\u201d That\u2019s a different relationship. And that\u2019s all that Green was trying to say. 2) I\u2019m so damned tired of your thoughts and prayers. It\u2019s time for those who have power and influence to do something about this mass murder madness! Don\u2019t give me \u201cwhat about Chicago?\u201d in response. Do something. Try something. Try anything. If it doesn\u2019t work, it doesn\u2019t work. Nothing is worth than your doing nothing, tragedy after tragedy. This is not \u201cthe cost of freedom.\u201d This is insanity. 3) So sad to read this , but hopefully, as Breanna Stewart hopes, someone else will be spared the horrific abuse she suffered as a result of her speaking out. 4) For all the caterwauling about lower NFL ratings, maybe the reason pro football\u2019s numbers are down this year is not because of backlash against player protests, but because so many high-profile stars \u2014 Aaron Rodgers, J.J. Watt, Odell Beckham, Jr., David Johnson, Andrew Luck and, last Thursday, Houston Texans rookie sensation quarterback Deshaun Watson \u2014 have suffered serious injuries and been out of action for large chunks of the season. * * * MORE MORNING TIP:  Young Lakers see bright future | DA\u2019s Top 15 Rankings | What\u2019s next for Okafor, Sixers? | Q&A: C.J. Antetokounmpo fired the first shot in this season\u2019s MVP chase while many of the usual suspects were trying to work out roster kinks or recover from a championship afterglow in an abbreviated training camp and preseason. Last season, after Antetokounmpo became just one of five players in NBA history to lead his team in points, rebounds, assists, steals and blocks for an entire season, there was no turning back. Now that his strength has improved to the point where he can\u2019t be so easily knocked off his spots offensively and he can score in so many different ways, without coming close to mastering his stroke beyond the 3-point line, he\u2019s basically unguardable. \u201cGiannis is truly a student of the game,\u201d Middleton said. \u201cHe listens to the coaches, they show him film and he\u2019s always in the gym working on his game and trying to find ways to get better. So I can only imagine how crazy it must be to you guys on the outside looking at it. \u201cBut trust me, what you are seeing \u2026 it\u2019s real.\u201d * * * The top five in the first edition of the 2017-18 Kia Race to the MVP Ladder: Season stats:  31.3 points per game, 10.6 rebounds per game, 5.3 assists per game His MVP case:  Antetokounmpo and the Bucks needed a day off after two rough outings: a home loss on Halloween to the Oklahoma City Thunder and a tough loss in Charlotte a night later . That isn\u2019t limited to players. Or to men.  Nearly one-third of the referees in the G League this season are women, and some \u2013 like dozens of players, coaches, front-office staff and refs \u2013 are moving closer to realizing NBA hopes. Every referee hired by the NBA in the last 15 years has some experience in what\u2019s now called the G League, which means it\u2019s a proving ground for both those who are making plays and making calls. \u201cIt was my experience that as my skill set deepened, so did my resolve to work at the highest level,\u201d said Lauren Holtkamp, the NBA\u2019s third full-time female referee and someone who spent six seasons in what was called the NBA Development League before it was rebranded earlier this year. \u201cThe training fueled the desire.\u201d That\u2019s the same sort of sentiment players in the G League have. Every NBA team opened this season with a G League alum on its roster, some with many of them. The NBA says 38 percent of its players right now have at least some G League experience, and that number has been rising rapidly in recent years. In 2004-05, there were 15 G League graduates starting the season in the NBA; this season, there were 167. Detroit, Atlanta, Toronto, Chicago and Miami combined to have 48 G League alums on their rosters \u2013 more than the entire NBA had a decade ago. \u201cI absolutely think all are really realizing the high level of talent, the high level of competition that we have in the league,\u201d G League President Malcolm Turner said. \u201cWe are a league of opportunity and a league of aspiration. We can be a launch pad to help NBA dreams come true whether you\u2019re a player, coach, referee, on down the line and that\u2019s reflected in the style of play that you see. Our guys get after it, night in and night out.\u201d The league is now up to a record 26 teams; a 27th is coming next season and Turner hasn\u2019t ruled out the chance that the league\u2019s goal \u2013 30 teams, all single-affiliated with an NBA club \u2013 could become reality within a year or two at the most. It\u2019s also the first season with two-way contract opportunities for players, who can spend up to 45 days in the NBA on those more-lucrative deals while continuing to develop in the G League. The G League will also again be a laboratory of sorts for the NBA. To start this season, the G League is using four-person refereeing crews instead of the customary three, plus will be offering a coach\u2019s challenge and a 2-minute overtime. As has been the case with plenty of other experiments over the years, the NBA will study what happens and see if the results suggest it could help their game. But when it comes to the female referees, that\u2019s no experiment. Many worked summer league games this year, and Holtkamp \u2013 the only woman working NBA games as an official right now \u2013 is looking forward to the day when female refs might no longer be viewed as a novelty. \u201cI\u2019m invigorated knowing there are talented and determined women in the developmental program who have access to the best training in the world,\u201d Holtkamp said. \u201cI believe in their ability to do this work.\u201d"},{"id":43379,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/nba-hold-lottery-chicago-next-spring","date":"2017-11-03","title":"NBA to hold lottery in Chicago next spring","content":" CHICAGO (AP) \u2014 The NBA says it will hold its draft lottery in Chicago next spring. The Blazers had to trade Allen Crabbe this summer, but are hoping a full season of Nurkic with Damian Lillard and C.J. McCollum will help them break a string of three straight seasons where they lost more games than they did the season before. They started off the season right, beating the Phoenix Suns by 48 points. Since then, the results have been mixed, though the Blazers could be 7-1 if not for the late-game heroics of Giannis Antetokounmpo ( three big defensive plays in the final minute ) and Blake Griffin ( game-winning three at the buzzer ), as well as a 1-for-7 finish at the end of regulation in Utah on Wednesday. Through eight games, the Blazers\u2019 record (4-4) belies their point differential ( plus-56 ), which is that of a 6-2 team. Of course, the point differential is somewhat skewed by a 48-point win in Phoenix on opening night. Since then, the Blazers are 3-4 with a point differential (plus-8) of a team that\u2019s 4-3. Wednesday\u2019s overtime loss was the lone road game in an 11-game stretch that continues with the Blazers\u2019 longest homestand of the season, six games over the next two weeks. Portland will try to get back over .500 with a win over the 3-4 Los Angeles Lakers at 10:30 ET in the second game of TNT\u2019s Thursday double-header. Pace : 100.0 (21st) OffRtg : 105.2 (13th) DefRtg : 99.1 (5th) NetRtg : +6.0 (7th) Team:  Per game stats | Advanced splits | Lineups | Game log Players:  Per game stats | On-off court | Player shooting History:  Season by season results | Advanced stats | Franchise leaders The Blazers rank last in field goal percentage in the restricted area at 47 percent. This isn\u2019t anything new. The Blazers ranked 28th in restricted-area field goal percentage last season and 16th (out of 16 teams) in the 2017 playoffs . Still, shooting less than 50 percent within a few feet of the basket is pretty brutal. The league average last season was 61 percent, giving restricted area shots the highest value (1.22 points per attempt) on the floor. There are six players who have shot less than 50 percent on at least 25 shots in the restricted area , and three of the six \u2013 Maurice Harkless (45 percent), Damian Lillard (47 percent) and Jusuf Nurkic (48 percent) are in Blazers\u2019 starting lineup. A fourth starter \u2013 C.J. McCollum (7-for-20) \u2013 has been the third worst shooter in the restricted area among 128 players with at least 20 attempts . The fifth starter \u2013 Al-Farouq Aminu (10-for-18) \u2013 is also below the league average. The Blazers get a good percentage of their shots in the restricted area, and part of that total is tip-in and put-back attempts. The Blazers are tied for second in offensive rebounding percentage and lead the league in the percentage of their possessions that are put-backs , according to Synergy play-type tracking. The quality of those shots can vary. Some tip-ins are more controlled than others. Even at 47 percent, the Blazers\u2019 restricted-area shots are worth more than their shots between the restricted area and the 3-point line, on which they\u2019ve shot a better-than-average 36 percent. McCollum has been one of the league\u2019s best shooters on non-restricted-area 2-point shots, but over the course of his career his layups (shot at 52 percent) have still been worth more than his other twos (46 percent). Still, an inability to get easy points at the rim puts more pressure on the Blazers to make jumpers. Since the start of last season, the Blazers are 33-17 when they\u2019ve had an *effective field goal percentage of 50 percent or better on shots from outside the paint, and 12-28 when they haven\u2019t. * Effective field goal percentage = (FGM + (0.5 * 3PM)) \/ FGA The Blazers have missed seven of their eight shots in the restricted area with the game within five points in the last five minutes. That makes for some frustrating moments. Through Wednesday, Portland ranks 26th in overall effective field goal percentage (48.5 percent), even though its ranks sixth from outside the paint (52.5 percent). The Blazers\u2019 restricted-area shooting should rise above 50 percent in the next few weeks, but their inability to finish will continue to haunt them from time to time. Below is a possession where Nurkic sets three screens. The final one frees Evan Turner for a foul-line jumper and draws Alex Len to the ball, which allows Nurkic to get rebounding position under the basket. But he misses the tip. Fairly simple, but also a prototypical Blazers miss in the restricted area. Another common theme among their misses is Lillard going with the high degree of difficulty (bonus missed tip-in from Harkless)\u2026 \u2022 NBA.com\/stats Video:  Watch the Blazers shoot 9-for-22 in the restricted area against the Clippers on Oct. 26 1. The Blazers have allowed 8.7 fewer points per 100 possessions than they did last season . That makes them the fourth most improved defensive team in the league through Wednesday. But if you throw away the opening-night win in Phoenix, the improvement isn\u2019t so drastic and they rank 12th defensively (102.8 points allowed per 100 possessions) instead of fifth (99.1) . Still, four of their eight games have been against teams \u2013 the Pacers, Bucks, Clippers and Raptors \u2013 that currently rank in the top 10 in offensive efficiency . Other teams have played easier schedules in that regard, and the Blazers held both Indiana and Toronto under their season-long marks for points scored per 100 possessions. Portland leads the league in opponent effective field goal percentage . For the second straight season, they lead the league in opponent field goal percentage in the restricted area. But a big chunk of their improvement has come in opponent 3-point percentage , which we shouldn\u2019t put much faith in at this point in the season. Also, it should be a little concerning that they\u2019ve had the league\u2019s seventh biggest increase in the percentage of opponent shots that come from the restricted area or 3-point range. You can better control what shots your opponents take, and the Blazers haven\u2019t been as good at forcing the less-efficient ones. But the Blazers have also seen a big improvement in defensive rebounding percentage, ranking in the top five after ranking 12th last season (and 10th after the All-Star break ). 2. The Blazers have been 17.4 points per 100 possessions better in the second half of games (plus-16.2) than they\u2019ve been in the first half (minus-1.1) . That\u2019s the biggest such differential in the league through Wednesday. The Blazers have yet to lose the second half, having won it in their first seven games and played even with the Jazz in the second half on Wednesday. The bigger difference (11.1 points per 100 possessions) between the first-half Blazers and the second-half Blazers has been on offense. Lillard and McCollum have a combined effective field goal percentage of 43 percent in the first half and 56 percent in the second half. Nurkic has also shot much better in the second half of games ( 49 percent vs. 38 percent ). 3. The Blazers have been almost 28 points per 100 possessions better with Aminu on the floor (plus-16.6) than with him on the bench (minus-11.2). It\u2019s early and the Phoenix game has to be taken into account with everything, but Aminu has been Portland\u2019s plus-minus man this season. And that may be about who\u2019s on the floor when Terry Stotts is staggering the minutes of Lillard and McCollum. Last season, when McCollum went to the bench midway through the first and third quarters, Harkless was more likely to stay on the floor with Lillard. And last season, Harkless had the better on-off numbers than Aminu, who played more minutes with McCollum than with Lillard. This season, Aminu has played more minutes with Lillard and Nurkic than with McCollum and the Blazers\u2019 back-up bigs. The Blazers\u2019 most-used two-man combination has been Aminu and Lillard, who have played 233 minutes together. * * * John Schuhmann is a staff writer for NBA.com. Ira Winderman of the South Florida Sun-Sentinel has more : * * *"},{"id":48672,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/giannis-antetokounmpo-nikola-jokic-allow-nuggets-bucks-go-against-nba-grain","date":"2017-11-01","title":"Denver Nuggets, Milwaukee Bucks going against NBA's guard-heavy grain","content":" They\u2019ve become the must-have accessory in the NBA (just ahead of designer headphones and hoodie warmups), the one player no team can do without, the one player that no team seems to lack. Yes, quality point guards are a dime-a-dozen group now in the NBA. They\u2019re populating the league in such abundance that the Phoenix Suns didn\u2019t flinch when they told disgruntled starter Eric Bledsoe to stay home or the hair salon \u2014 whichever he preferred. It\u2019s hard to find a serious playoff contender that doesn\u2019t have one (and some have two). And then there are the Denver Nuggets and Milwaukee Bucks, who arguably have none. Partly by necessity and partly by choice, both teams are running their offenses through gifted big men and getting reasonably decent results. These two teams are building for a big run while also going against the NBA\u2019s trend \u2026 and, by no coincidence, are the two most logical landing spots for Bledsoe in a trade. Pump the brakes, though. Neither seems to be in a rush because they\u2019re weighing the merits of using young, non-traditional point guards as compliments to the centerpieces: Giannis Antetokoumnpo with the Bucks and Nikola Jokic with the Nuggets. Both are solid passers and act as triggers while their point guards orbit around them, defer to them and pick spots to command the ball. But when, if ever, will either team get cold feet and fall in line with the rest of the NBA? The Suns would like to know, but it could be a long wait if the Bucks get the right results from reigning Kia Rookie of the Year Malcolm Brogdon and the Nuggets likewise from Jamal Murray. Their teams are taking a wait-and-see approach with their development while leaning heavily on Antetokounmpo and Jokic\u2019s respective playmaking. Their coaches are saying all the right things: Jason Kidd of the Bucks: \u201cMalcolm knows how to play the right way. He\u2019s getting better. We\u2019re lucky to have him.\u201d Michael Malone of the Nuggets: \u201cI believe in him and (Murray) has to believe in himself.\u201d Yet both coaches are acutely aware that Murray and Brogdon, because of their size, can also play off the ball. Murray, for one, might be better suited as a game-finisher anyway. Both teams are in play for Bledsoe or perhaps a veteran addition either at the trade deadline or in free agency next summer. Brogdon surprised the NBA in winning Rookie of the Year while Ben Simmons missed last season and Joel Embiid played only 31 games. Still, that doesn\u2019t diminish what Brogdon delivered last season and his value to the Bucks now. He\u2019s wiser than most NBA sophomores because he stayed all four years in college and, as a second-round pick, his sense of urgency and hunger was greater than that of lottery picks. Brogdon is a self-made grinder, a consistent player who rarely screws up and is already one of the Bucks\u2019 better defenders. The Bucks know what they\u2019re getting from him on a nightly basis. \u201cI\u2019m a lot more confident,\u201d Brogdon said. \u201cWhen you have a year of experience and also the experience of playing in the playoffs, it just makes a world of difference. I know what my role is. I feel I\u2019ve found my niche with this team.\u201d Yet, Brogdon\u2019s four assists per game (in 32.1 minutes per game) ranks 38th among starting point guards mainly because of Antetokoumnpo, who handles the ball and runs the offense much like LeBron James does. Brogdon\u2019s ability and willingness to blend with Antetokounmpo is helpful to a system that plays off the young superstar\u2019s multiple skills. Giannis is off to an MVP-like start and the last thing the Bucks want to do is slow his roll. But Kidd also wants Brogdon to sharpen his point guard instincts as well. \u201cWe talked about it last year, understanding when it\u2019s time to score, being able to play-make, understanding how to get a teammate a shot, just being consistent when learning how to run the show,\u201d Kidd said. \u201cHe\u2019s been able to run the offense and be a leader. \u201cAnd really, it\u2019s all about that, and understanding who hasn\u2019t touched the ball. That\u2019s what makes a point guard special in this league. Figure out how to get the ball to the right people at the right time. That\u2019s the next step for Malcolm.\u201d The Nuggets waited until the eve of the season to name their starter at point guard, although it was clear last year that Murray had pole position. He assumed the role late in the season from Emmanuel Mudiay ( who started 55 games ) and Jameer Nelson ( 40 starts ) and kept the ball, starting seven games. That wasn\u2019t the plan when the Nuggets took him No. 7 overall in the 2016 Draft . Mudiay was their point guard of the future and Murray, who didn\u2019t play the position in college at Kentucky, was projected as a scoring guard. But Mudiay\u2019s erratic shooting, limited range and inconsistent playmaking opened up the job, which Murray won almost by default after the Nuggets waived Nelson . Malone admitted that Muray\u2019s edge on Mudiay, a superior athlete, was shooting. Malone wanted someone with deeper range next to Gary Harris to space the floor for Jokic and newcomer Paul Millsap. Problem is, Murray\u2019s shooting ( 37.1 percent ) has been Mudiay-like here in the early season. From Oct. 21-27, he missed 16 straight 3-pointers and is making just 18.2 percent of his 3-pointers (after shooting 33.4 percent in 2016-17). His defense remains an issue at times ( 100.6 Defensive Rating this season ) and part of the Nuggets\u2019 slow start could be pinpointed to Murray\u2019s growing pains. \u201cI think they drafted me for a reason,\u201d Murray said. \u201cI just go out there and play basketball. I\u2019m not worried about missing. I just got to be thinking about the next shot.\u201d Malone and the Nuggets are taking the long view and realize Murray, 20, is trying to master NBA point guard play on the fly. But if they\u2019re anxious to make a significant move in the tough West this season, the Nuggets\u2019 point guard position might need an upgrade at starter or backup. \u201cHe showed me he\u2019s not afraid of the moment,\u201d Malone said, who added that part of the learning experience for players such as Murray means to deal with the not-so-good days and \u201clet them play through it.\u201d The Nuggets and Bucks are hesitant to include Murray or Brogdon in trade talks for good reason: Both are on cheap rookie deals and are big parts of each team\u2019s future. Teams rarely move players this quickly unless there\u2019s a serious issue ( think Chris Webber after his rookie season in Golden State ) or a deal is too good to skip. It wouldn\u2019t be a surprise if the Nuggets are trying instead to unload Mudiay in a package to Phoenix and the Bucks are selling some combination of John Henson and Matthew Dellavedova. There\u2019s risk, too, in acquiring Bledsoe himself. He went rogue with the Suns and teams usually shy away from players with flapping red flags. If he came to Milwaukee or Denver and didn\u2019t mesh with Giannis or Jokic, it would be a disaster. Until further notice, the Bucks and Nuggets are good to go with the status quo. Teams can gawk all they want at their lack of a true point guard \u2026 and then deal with the sight of a 6-foot-11 Antetokounmpo reaching the rim in three steps, or with the sight of 6-foot-10 Jokic throwing Bill Walton-like backdoor passes from the key. "},{"id":43411,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/kristaps-porzingis-fast-start-face-knicks","date":"2017-11-01","title":"Kristaps Porzingis not surprised by sizzling start as New York Knicks' main man","content":" NEW YORK (AP) \u2014 Kristaps Porzingis is neither surprised nor satisfied by his sensational start to the NBA season. He entered play Wednesday third in the league in scoring with 29.3 points per game after the best beginning stretch ever for a New York Knicks player. He already was the first player in franchise history to score 30 in four of his first five games, then went for a career-best 38 in a victory over Denver on Monday. The Knicks made him the face of the franchise when they traded Carmelo Anthony, and he looks up for the challenge. \u201cI\u2019m never surprised by my own game,\u201d Porzingis said. \u201cI know what I can do on the basketball court. I always expect the most out of myself.\u201d He steadied the Knicks after a 0-3 start by leading them to three straight victories heading into their game Wednesday against Houston. The extra attention that defenses can pay him now that Anthony is gone hasn\u2019t helped them stop the 7-foot-3 forward. He spent the summer training in Latvia, leading his home country to a quarterfinal finish in the European championships. It appeared that he would return to New York as the No. 2 option, then he became the main man when the Knicks dealt Anthony to Oklahoma City on the eve of training camp. Now Porzingis is scoring in a way even beyond what Anthony did. \u201cI put in so much work and for me it\u2019s not a surprise that I\u2019m able to play at this level right now,\u201d Porzingis said. Porzingis knew he would need strength to get through the long NBA season after losing weight during it in his first two seasons. Searching for a way to keep the weight on, he began at the end of last season eating Zing Bars between meals, trying to meet his goal of eating around 5,000 calories a day. It\u2019s worked so far \u2013 Porzingis said he\u2019s around 243 pounds, about 20 up from his rookie season. And he became such a fan of the product that he decided to invest in the company and will be on its board of directors. \u201cI told them if I\u2019m in, I want to be involved 100 percent and I want to know also the business part of it, not just put my face on the bar and that\u2019s it,\u201d Porzingis said. Those kinds of opportunities will be more available to him now as the marquee player in the biggest market. He wanted to take advantage of them when he arrived as a rookie but quickly realized it took up too much of his time so he cut back, preferring to focus solely on just a few partnerships. He said that hasn\u2019t changed. \u201cThe rest of the stuff will come and it\u2019s already coming,\u201d Porzingis said. \u201cThere\u2019s a lot of stuff that we say no to just because we want to keep basketball as the main priority, and I need my rest days and I need time off and I can\u2019t be doing stuff every day off. So we really are just doing the stuff that I really want to be involved with.\u201d Porzingis seems well on his way to a first All-Star berth \u2013 though fans at Madison Square Garden were thinking beyond that when they showered him with \u201cMVP! MVP!\u201d chants on Monday, a night after he led the Knicks to a surprising victory in Cleveland. He may not continue scoring at this level, but he thinks he can keep playing that way. \u201cI believe so,\u201d he said. \u201cThat\u2019s the goal. Or are these early struggles something Cavs fans should be frightened by?  * * * David Aldridge :  Call me in April. Seen this movie too many times to be moved by Cleveland\u2019s defensive indifference in October. The age of this year\u2019s top Cavs, combined with the shorter preseason, does lead one to believe that they\u2019re not in great shape yet, and players that use the season to try and improve their conditioning tend to be susceptible to injury. So we need to keep an eye on that. But the only long-term significant question for Cleveland is what Isaiah Thomas shows up on the floor when he returns from his hip injury. If it\u2019s Boston Isaiah \u2014 an elite scorer with devastating fourth-quarter closeout ability \u2014 the Cavs will be fine. That Isaiah is the perfect complement to LeBron and Kevin Love, and will make Cleveland impossible to stop in a seven-game series. But the limited Isaiah of the Eastern Conference finals last spring would be nothing but a target for whoever the Cavs play in June. Steve Aschburner : More concerned about than frightened by, I think. I was counting on all the new faces in Cleveland to grab the Cavaliers\u2019 attention this season in a way last year\u2019s returning crew never fully engaged with its first 82. Between adapting and adjusting to Derrick Rose, Dwyane Wade and Jae Crowder, then shifting gears again when Isaiah Thomas returns, I thought that \u2014 and an alleged MVP push by Kyrie-inspired LeBron James \u2014 would carry the Cavs through these six months. They remain my pick to reach The Finals but this lackluster start only emboldens challengers, from near-contenders in Boston, Washington and Toronto to early-round upstarts like Milwaukee or, given history and matchups, even Detroit. Golden State can get away with this in a way Cleveland cannot. Shaun Powell :  This start seems bizarre, and the wipeouts suffered by the likes of the Brooklyn Nets downright unreal. Usually the Cavs wait until March to become bored; maybe they\u2019re just getting it out of the way early this year. Of course, I tend to ignore pretty much anything weird that happens before Thanksgiving. Besides missing Isaiah Thomas, the Cavs don\u2019t feel the need to finish with the best record in the East \u2014 or the top two, for that matter. They need to stay healthy. If LeBron was hurt, then I\u2019d be frightened for Cavs fans. John Schuhmann : Last season taught us that the regular-season Cavs and the playoff Cavs are two very different teams. But this season\u2019s Cavs are different too, and while defensive malaise is to be expected, there\u2019s reason to be concerned about the offense. They\u2019ve added three guys \u2014 Jeff Green, Derrick Rose and Dwyane Wade \u2014 who have shot a combined 28 percent from 3-point range over the last three-plus seasons. That\u2019s not the best way to complement LeBron James. Floor spacing and 3-point shooting have been the Cavs\u2019 bread and butter over the last three seasons and both have been compromised with the changes they\u2019ve made. So far (small sample size alert), the Cavs have been better offensively with none of those guys on the floor (108 points scored per 100 possessions) than they\u2019ve been with one (105) or two (100). They have time to figure out a new way to succeed offensively, but it\u2019s certainly not a sure thing that they\u2019ll be as good as they were last season.  Sekou Smith : When the Cavaliers were younger, from top to bottom, their temporary struggles didn\u2019t bother me as much. You knew there was method to whatever madness a LeBron James-led team was going through and that he would get them through it. LeBron is still doing LeBron-like things \u2026 but he\u2019s working with a much older crew than normal. So there could be cause for concern, and Cavaliers coach Tyronn Lue has said as much. I don\u2019t frighten as easily, though, so I\u2019m in no rush to push the panic button on the Cavaliers. If they don\u2019t have these issue solved by Thanksgiving, then I\u2019ll be ready to take another look. Yes, the core of this team has played to the final game in each of the past three seasons \u2014 so there has to be some mental, physical and emotional wear-and-tear involved. That\u2019s no excuse. Shaun Powell :  I\u2019ll go with Embiid-Simmons with the understandable qualifier of \u201cif they stay healthy.\u201d Embiid can be just as good if not better than Karl-Anthony Towns, while Simmons is massively multi-dimensional and this gives him the edge on the others. Sekou Smith :  Embiid and Simmons are downright frightening when you factor in the size for their position, raw talent and injury history for both of these young superstars in training. John Schuhmann : The Magic have the much better point differential, but their five wins are against teams that are otherwise 15-15, while the Pistons\u2019 five wins (highlighted by back-to-back road wins over the Clippers and Warriors) are over teams that are otherwise 20-9. Sekou Smith : Right now it\u2019s fun to see both of these teams we didn\u2019t necessarily expect to be playoff contenders come out of the gate as strong as they have. Last season taught us to ignore (for the most part) the struggles of the regular-season Cleveland Cavaliers. But these Cavs are different than last season\u2019s Cavs, with three new rotation pieces \u2013 Derrick Rose, Dwyane Wade and Jeff Green \u2013 that don\u2019t necessarily fit well alongside LeBron James. It\u2019s fair to discount any losses the Cavs incur before April. It\u2019s also fair to wonder about Cleveland\u2019s ability to succeed while counting on those three guys. In the words of Sam Hinkie, every game is a data point. The Golden State Warriors have much more continuity than the Cavs, retaining the top eight guys from the team that went 16-1 in the postseason. So their three losses are easier to brush aside. But those three losses still make it impossible to put the champs at the top of the Power Rankings. The difficulty of putting the league\u2019s 30 teams in order goes well beyond that. Of the 30, 22 are no better than 4-2 and no worse than 2-4. Seven teams are 3-3, while one team\u2019s only win is another team\u2019s only loss. We\u2019ve got some serious parity early on and it might take some time before things out. Forgive us if these rankings look silly a month from now and, for now, focus on the team-by-team details, rather than the big picture. * * * Pace : Possessions per 48 minutes (League Rank) OffRtg : Points scored per 100 possessions (League Rank) DefRtg : Points allowed per 100 possessions (League Rank) NetRtg : Point differential per 100 possessions (League Rank) The league has averaged 101.2 possessions (per team) per 48 minutes and 103.4 points scored per 100 possessions this season. * * * NBA.com\u2019s Power Rankings, released every Monday during the season, are just one man\u2019s opinion. If you have an issue with the rankings, or have a question or comment for John Schuhmann, send him an e-mail or contact him via Twitter . * * *"},{"id":43465,"link":"https:\/\/www.nba.com\/news\/morning-tip-al-harrington-becomes-medical-marijuana-advocate-businessman","date":"2017-10-30","title":"Al Harrington wants to help NBA see benefits of medical marijuana","content":"He swears that this came to him later in life, when he\u2019d tried everything else. \u201cAnd I\u2019m not lying; I was one of those guys who grew up thinking if you smoked weed you had no future,\u201d Al Harrington says. \u201cI never tried it as a kid. My boys were smoking when we were kids and I never tried it. I was always so scared to try it. I felt like if I came home and I smelled like weed, my mother would kill me.\u201d But toward the end of a 16-year career that saw him go from \u201cBaby Al\u201d as a high school Draft pick (No. 25 overall) by Indiana in 1998 through seven NBA stops, Harrington was dealing with the same chronic knee and joint pain that follows just about every player who stays with it long enough. And he had a knee operation that led to a staph infection while playing for Denver in 2012 left Harrington in severe pain. The anti-inflammatories he\u2019d been taking regularly the previous seven and a half years weren\u2019t having much effect. Harrington then thought about trying medical marijuana. And, fortunately, he lived in a state that was one of the first to legalize use of marijuana for medicinal purposes. Colorado residents approved Amendment 20 in 2000 , which legalized medical marijuana use for patients who got explicit medical consent. Voters then passed Amendment 64 in November, 2012, which led to full legalization in 2014. Adults 21 and over could grow and possess limited amounts of marijuana for recreational use. \u201cI didn\u2019t have to do much research because I was in Denver when everything started to go legal,\u201d Harrington said. \u201cI\u2019m one of those guys that reads the paper the next day to see what they said about the team. And it was always in the paper, every single day. So I was always reading up on it, reading up on it.\u201d Harrington began using the medicinal form of the drugs, in creams that he rubbed on his knees, and drops. They didn\u2019t bring the high that smoking marijuana delivers. (Much more on that below.) But it brought Harrington relief. He says that after using anti-inflammatory pills for seven and a half years, he hasn\u2019t had to use one since. And it opened his eyes to the possibilities that medical marijuana could bring to people in pain. And after he retired in 2014 ( he did play in Ice Cube\u2019s Big3 league this past summer ), Harrington has gone into cannabis full-time, leading to the development of marijuana businesses in three states and a dream of being a major mogul in the still-burgeoning racket of legalized medical marijuana. His company is called Viola Extracts \u2014 Viola is Harrington\u2019s grandmother, and, as he describes it, his \u201cguinea pig\u201d in the struggle. When Viola Harrington\u2019s glaucoma was eased by using medical marijuana a couple of years ago, Al Harrington became a true believer. Now Viola Extracts is a one-stop grower, distributing and selling to more than 30 legal marijuana stores around the country. And Harrington now thinks medical marijuana should be available for use in another location \u2014 the NBA. And his view is now supported, publicly, by the unlikeliest of parties. \u201cI\u2019m now at the point where, personally, I think it probably should be removed from the banned list,\u201d former NBA Commissioner David Stern told Harrington in a segment for a piece on Harrington\u2019s new business that aired last week on Uninterrupted, the multimedia platform for athletes launched in 2015 by LeBron James. (Full disclosure: Turner Sports, which runs this website on which you\u2019ve stumbled, is an investor in Uninterrupted.) \u201cYou\u2019ve persuaded me,\u201d Stern told Harrington, after the two had met a couple of times earlier this year to discuss the pros and cons of medicinal marijuana. \u201c\u2026 If you told the fans that if the players rubbed it (marijuana creams) on their knees that they wouldn\u2019t take a night off, that would really send it over the top.\u201d Marijuana has been on the NBA\u2019s banned substances list since 1999 . A first positive test for marijuana requires enrollment in a treatment program, along with aftercare testing. A second positive test results in a $25,000 fine, and a third positive results in a five-game suspension, with each subsequent positive test thereafter adding five more games to the previous suspension; i.e., 10 games for a fourth positive test, 15 games for a fifth, etc. The NBA\u2019s Executive Vice President of Communications, Mike Bass, gave this statement to USA Today \u2019s Jeff Zilgitt last week: \u201cwhile (current NBA) commissioner (Adam) Silver has said that we are interested in better understanding the safety and efficacy of medical marijuana, our position remains unchanged regarding the use by current NBA players of marijuana for recreational purposes.\u201d Bass said last Thursday that this remains the NBA\u2019s official statement on Stern\u2019s comments. But Silver\u2019s position on medicinal marijuana is a little more nuanced. He said during a trip to Israel in August: \u201cI would say it\u2019s something we will look at. I\u2019m very interested in the science when it comes to medical marijuana. My personal view is that it should be regulated in the same way that other medications are if the plan is to use it for pain management. And it\u2019s something that needs to be discussed with our Players Association, but to the extent that science demonstrates that there are effective uses for medical reasons, we\u2019ll be open to it. Hopefully there\u2019s not as much pain involved in our sport as some others, so there\u2019s not as much need for it.\u201d Stern\u2019s shift dovetails with a decided move in public opinion toward increased legalization of marijuana. The NBA, after applying great public pressure, got the National Basketball Players Association to agree to add marijuana to the banned list in \u201999. Entering the post-Michael Jordan era (at least, that\u2019s what everyone thought at the time; Jordan had retired following the 1998 season, only to return to the court with the Wizards in 2001), the league had public perception and keeping its paying customers and sponsors happy on its mind. And after the arrests of several high-profile players for marijuana-related charges, the NBA made a marijuana ban a priority. There were also, sotto voce , players who were concerned about the prevalence of use among their brethren. But now, 29 states and the District of Columbia allow medical marijuana use . Those states are home to 18 of the NBA\u2019s 30 teams \u2014 the Phoenix Suns, Golden State Warriors, Los Angeles Lakers, LA Clippers, Sacramento Kings, Denver Nuggets, Miami Heat, Orlando Magic, Chicago Bulls, Boston Celtics, Detroit Pistons, Minnesota Timberwolves, New York Knicks, Brooklyn Nets, Cleveland Cavaliers, Portland Trail Blazers, Philadelphia 76ers and Washington Wizards. Eight states \u2014 Alaska, Colorado, California, Maine, Massachusetts, Nevada, Oregon and Washington, along with D.C. \u2014 have legalized recreational use. And a Gallup Poll released just last week showed a record number of Americans \u2014 64 percent \u2014 now believe all marijuana use should be legal . That percentage is the highest in a Gallup survey since the company started polling on the issue in 1969. And the majority support cuts across party lines, with 72 percent of Democrats, 67 percent of Independents and 51 percent of Republicans supporting decriminalization. Warriors coach Steve Kerr said he tried medical marijuana last year to try and ease the chronic back pain he\u2019s suffered with for the past two years . Former Knicks president and Bulls coach Phil Jackson said on the CBS Sports Network program \u201cWe Need to Talk\u201d last year that he smoked marijuana in the 1970s while recovering from back surgery , but did not know if it had medicinal value or not. \u201cI think it was a distraction for me as much as a pain reliever,\u201d Jackson said. \u201cBut I\u2019ve never thought of it as, ultimately, a pain medication for that type of situation. I know ocular things, stomach, digestive issues and other things, I think it\u2019s regarded quite highly.\u201d But it\u2019s high time (pun indented), Harrington believes, to get in on a business that generated an estimated $6.7 billion in revenue last year, and is projected to generate more than $20 billion in North America alone by 2021 . Harrington started in earnest in 2014, after providing plants for cancer patients and HIV patients in Colorado. By the time he applied for a license, he\u2019d discovered the Byzantine layers between states when it came to being a budding entrepreneur. \u201cThere\u2019s a lot of different restrictions in each state,\u201d Harrington said. \u201cEach state is governed differently. Some have residency requirements. Some are trying to monopolize the whole process, where they talk about giving out only five licenses. Some states are equal opportunity. Every state is different and has different rules. But in Colorado, you had to be a resident. I was a resident because I was playing. But obviously I couldn\u2019t put it in my name because I didn\u2019t know how the NBA would even react to knowing I had a small piece of ownership in a marijuana company.\u201d Harrington\u2019s cousin, Dan, became the \u201cowner\u201d of the company, with Al Harrington the silent investor. Their company doesn\u2019t have much overhead. Though Harrington has hired a COO to get some of the day-to-day business off his plate, he and his cousin do a lot of the scut work throughout the process. \u201cWe\u2019re vertically integrated for the most part,\u201d he said. \u201cWe cultivate, manufacture, package, handle ourselves \u2026 I travel more than I did when I played in the league. I gambled on myself and I put everything on my own plate. I\u2019ve learned the industry. \u201cI\u2019ve had a lot of people that were interested in joining my operation and different things like that, but for me, being an entrepreneur and doing this for the first time, I wanted to learn everything on my own without other people influencing and different things like that. Even as far as raising money, I\u2019ve just gotten to the point in almost five years where I\u2019m comfortable raising money, because I know how exactly I can get everyone\u2019s money back. I know how to grow this business now.\u201d Harrington is not only a rarity in the medical marijuana because of his former day job, but because of his ethnicity. Though some states, including Michigan and Massachusetts, have tried to open up the marijuana marketplace for minority businesses , the vast majority of marijuana dispensaries in the United States are owned by whites . This is relevant for a simple reason: the overwhelming majority of people arrested for marijuana possession and\/or distribution are people of color , and have been for a generation . And, in most states, anyone who\u2019s ever been convicted of a drug felony is ineligible to work in marijuana stores or processing plants. Harrington had money, so he had access. But he knows that others have been shut out. He\u2019s a member of the Minority Cannabis Business Association , which seeks to increase minority representation among cannabis company owners, employees and investors, and educate minority populations about cannabis use. \u201cWhen you think of the social justice issues and the war on drugs, at the end of the day let\u2019s keep it all the way real \u2014 it\u2019s against black and brown,\u201d Harrington said. \u201cI tell people all the time, if you look at the black market, the black market, their biggest clientele is college campuses. And you never see no bust on college campuses. But you go to every project, every \u2018hood in America where there\u2019s black and brown, and you see all these busts. It\u2019s really messed up, because they block you out. If you have a felony, if you have a small felony charge as a kid, a dime bag of marijuana, you can\u2019t even work in the industry. If you have a felony, you can\u2019t get a job in the industry, let alone own a business. \u201cAnd then you look at some of the other things. At the end of the day, even with black people or minorities in general, we\u2019re very creative. I feel like we influence culture. What we do not have is access to capital. And these states are requiring you to, they\u2019re saying, okay, you can apply for a license, but as part of the licensing process, you have to show a million dollars of working capital in an account before we\u2019ll even look at your license or your application. It\u2019s just messed up. \u201cMy whole thing is, it should be an environment where you can allow them to apply for the application, then allow them the opportunity to go out and raise money, or give them some opportunities to be able to get some capital so they can take advantage of this opportunity. At the end of the day now this industry is being built on all the minorities\u2019 backs, and now this is a new industry. To keep it real, all the white people are trying to cash in on it.\u201d Harrington\u2019s company specializes in extraction, pulling the various parts of the marijuana plant out for use as waxes or oils. He believes in the part of the plant that produces the compound CBD \u2014 cannabidiol \u2014 which has anti-inflammatory benefits. Other cannabinoids with similar pain-relieving properties are CBN (cannabinol) and CBG (cannabigerol). \u201cTHC (tetrahydrocannabinol) is what gets everybody high,\u201d Harrington said. \u201cIt works with CBD. It\u2019s like a perfect combination where you can combine the two. But I also understand that some people don\u2019t want that high feeling. So when you use CBD, you don\u2019t feel anything when you take it; you just start to feel the relief of whatever you\u2019re dealing with.\u201d Harrington says he never tested positive for marijuana even though he was using both medical marijuana creams on his knees and taking between 25 and 50 milligrams of marijuana drops daily the last two years of his career. \u201cThe last year, when I was in Washington, I was almost hoping that I tested dirty because I wanted to be able to open it up to the program people and be like, look, this is what I\u2019m taking,\u201d he said. \u201cBut I took it all those times and I never tested dirty. I had even asked do they test for CBD and they said yes, and I promise you, I was taking it every single day, and I never tested dirty on one of my tests. And it didn\u2019t give me no advantages or anything like that; it just gave me the pain relief that I needed to be able to perform.\u201d Harrington says he\u2019s spoken with NBPA President Chris Paul on several occasions in recent months to see if the union is willing or ready to make a push toward easing the current substance ban to at least include medical marijuana use. But changing the current policy would not be that simple; potential Pandora\u2019s Boxes are strewn about. Even though the 29 states and D.C. have legalized medical marijuana use, it is still, technically, a violation of federal law \u2014 the Controlled Substances Act of 1970 , which does not differentiate between recreational and medical marijuana. The Drug Enforcement Administration still considers marijuana a \u201cSchedule 1\u201d drug, defined as such: So, even if the NBPA and league were to agree to allow medical marijuana use by players, no one knows for sure what would happen afterward. Could a player using medical marijuana that was approved by his team still be arrested under federal law? Would players thus be held to a higher standard than other people in those states? After cracking down on medical marijuana developers earlier in the Barack Obama presidency , the Obama Administration did an about face , saying in 2013 that it would not challenge laws legalizing marijuana in Colorado and Washington state, and generally wasn\u2019t interested in prosecuting people for simple possession. But the Trump Administration has taken a much harder line. Attorney General Jeff Sessions said last month that \u201cFederal law remains in effect\u201d with regard to marijuana use, and made clear that he is not a supporter of more progressive policies with regard to the drug. \u201cI\u2019ve never felt that we should legalize marijuana,\u201d Sessions said last month. \u201cIt doesn\u2019t strike me that the country would be better if it\u2019s being sold on every street corner. We do know that legalization results in greater use.\u201d There also isn\u2019t a monolithic point of view among players. There are, of course, many players who support its use, both recreationally and for medical purposes. Others don\u2019t support drug use under any circumstances (the \u201cmy body is my temple\u201d folks). Others wonder why the NBPA signed off on testing for marijuana while other sports league do not. And there are players who wonder why marijuana is banned in some states, yet legal in others. \u201cI think its fair to say we have to be mindful that given the current administration and Sessions\u2019 comments on his view, that it\u2019s a gateway drug, it wouldn\u2019t be prudent for any changes to be made until we know what the current DOJ has to say about this,\u201d NBPA Executive Director Michele Roberts said Sunday. \u201cThe other sports that aren\u2019t testing for marijuana like we are, I don\u2019t know if they\u2019re paying attention to today\u2019s DOJ. I want us to be aware of what the political agenda looks like.\u201d Harrington not only wants to grow and sell medical marijuana, he is an advocate of it as a \u201cclean\u201d drug compared with the painkillers developed in pharmaceutical labs. \u201cThere\u2019s clinical risk in every pain medication they give you,\u201d he said. \u201cAnd they give you so much of it and (they\u2019re) so irresponsible. All these side effects. But when you look at marijuana, marijuana is a miracle drug. It heals \u2026 and I just want to get it out there. \u201cBecause like I said, I grew up with that mindset that it was just the worst thing in the world. But when you think about it, it came from God. It\u2019s a plant. There\u2019s no other drug that you can go outside, in your back yard, drop seeds and just let it grow. How is that a drug? Explain it to me. Why is that in the same classification as heroin and cocaine? That makes no sense to me.\u201d At the least, he\u2019d like to have it available for players who otherwise gobble anti-inflammatories by the handful. The shorthand has been well-known in NBA locker rooms for decades: Naprosyn, Celebrex, Vicodin, Orudis, Vioxx, Indocin and on and on. \u201cAll I\u2019m asking for is testing,\u201d Harrington said. \u201cCan we find out if this stuff really is as impactful, as a miracle drug, that most people, I feel, and most of the other patients feel about this? At this point, my mom is part of the Mother\u2019s Association. I\u2019m helping out like five, 10 moms right now with different things \u2014 knee replacements, things like that. And it\u2019s working.\u201d Stern \u2014 who was talking only about taking medical marijuana off the league\u2019s banned substances list, not making recreational use legal in the NBA \u2014 told Harrington in the Undefeated interview that he believed testing would begin in earnest once the world\u2019s major pharmaceutical companies figure out how they could monetize medical marijuana to their benefit. Harrington recalled a meeting a friend of his had with billionaire investor Warren Buffett; the friend wanted $850 million to start a movie studio. \u201cAnd Warren Buffett said, \u2018for our company, if it\u2019s not a $10 billion deal, we don\u2019t even look at it,\u2019 \u201d Harrington said. \u201cI think that\u2019s what\u2019s happening with Pharma. Pharma, of course, is going to take over \u2026 but it\u2019s not enough money (now) for them to waste their time. Once it gets to $50 billion, $100 billion, that\u2019s when they\u2019ll come in and buy all the established people. So that\u2019s why I firmly believe we have a five- or 10-year window to build our brand and build our businesses before Big Pharma comes in and the big money really starts coming in and they start buying people up.\u201d So Harrington has time to make his mark. He bought acres of land in Oregon in March, and just had his first harvest; his products will be on shelves out there by the end of this week. He\u2019s doing sales runs, meeting his sales teams and dispensary owners, and telling them his company\u2019s story. And he plans to keep working his former employer to change its mind. \u201cI think we\u2019re getting closer,\u201d Harrington said. \u201cWe are getting younger (NBA) owners. I personally feel a lot of those owners actually consumed, at different times, in different ways \u2014 edibles. I don\u2019t care. They can say what they want. I know what it is. Because we were NBA players, we\u2019re around a lot of bililionaires and some of the most successful people in the world, and when I talk to them about marijuana, the first thing they say is \u2018send me some.\u2019 \u201d * * * MORE MORNING TIP: DA\u2019s Top 15 rankings | Hawks embrace season of change | Morning Tip mailbag: How far can Pelicans go? * * * Longtime NBA reporter, columnist and Naismith Memorial Basketball Hall of Famer David Aldridge is an analyst for TNT.
